0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left">Related topics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Alt-right" title="Alt-right">Alt-right</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Anti-fascism" 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Portal:Fascism" title="Portal:Fascism">Fascism portal</a></li>\n<li><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872444996">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Fascism_sidebar" title="Template:Fascism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Fascism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Fascism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Fascism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>National Socialism</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Nationalsozialismus</i>), more commonly known as <b>Nazism</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;/: \'a\' in \'father\'">&#593;&#720;</span><span title="\'t\' in \'tie\'">t</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span><span title="/i/: \'y\' in \'happy\'">i</span><span title="/&#618;/: \'i\' in \'kit\'">&#618;</span><span title="\'z\' in \'zoom\'">z</span><span title="/&#601;m/: \'m\' in \'rhythm\'">&#601;m</span></span>, <wbr/><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/&#230;/: \'a\' in \'bad\'">&#230;</span><span title="\'t\' in \'tie\'">t</span></span>-/</a></span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2003_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2003-1">[1]</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a> and practices associated with the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> &#8211; officially the National Socialist German Workers\' Party (<i>Nationalsozialistische Deutsche Arbeiterpartei</i> or NSDAP) &#8211; in <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, and of other <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a> groups with similar aims.\n</p><p>Nazism is a form of <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a> and showed that ideology\'s disdain for <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary system</a>, but also incorporated fervent <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">scientific racism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a> into its creed. Its extreme <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> came from <a href="/wiki/Pan-Germanism" title="Pan-Germanism">Pan-Germanism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkisch_movement" title="V&#246;lkisch movement">V&#246;lkisch movement</a> prominent in the <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism" title="German nationalism">German nationalism</a> of the time, and it was strongly influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-Communist</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Freikorps" title="Freikorps">Freikorps</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> groups that emerged after Germany\'s defeat in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, from which came the party\'s "cult of violence" which was "at the heart of the movement."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nazism subscribed to theories of <a href="/wiki/Racial_hierarchy" title="Racial hierarchy">racial hierarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">Social Darwinism</a>, identifying the Germans as a part of what the Nazis regarded as an <a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nordic_race" title="Nordic race">Nordic</a> <a href="/wiki/Master_race" title="Master race">master race</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baum2006_156_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baum2006_156-3">[3]</a></sup> It aimed to overcome social divisions and create a German homogeneous society based on racial purity which represented a people\'s community (<i><a href="/wiki/Volksgemeinschaft#Nazi_Volksgemeinschaft" title="Volksgemeinschaft">Volksgemeinschaft</a></i>). The Nazis aimed to unite all Germans living in historically German territory, as well as gain additional lands for German expansion under the doctrine of <i><a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a></i> and exclude those who they deemed either community aliens or "inferior" races.\n</p><p>The term "National Socialism" arose out of attempts to create a nationalist redefinition of "socialism", as an alternative to both <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">international socialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market capitalism</a>. Nazism rejected the <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> concept of <a href="/wiki/Class_conflict" title="Class conflict">class conflict</a>, opposed <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitanism" title="Cosmopolitanism">cosmopolitan</a> <a href="/wiki/Internationalism_(politics)" title="Internationalism (politics)">internationalism</a>, and sought to convince all parts of the new German society to subordinate their personal interests to the "<a href="/wiki/Common_good" title="Common good">common good</a>", accepting political interests as the main priority of economic organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kobrak2004_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kobrak2004-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Nazi Party\'s precursor, the Pan-German nationalist and antisemitic <a href="/wiki/German_Workers%27_Party" title="German Workers\' Party">German Workers\' Party</a>, was founded on 5 January 1919. By the early 1920s the party was renamed the National Socialist German Workers\' Party &#8211; to attract workers away from left-wing parties such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Social_Democratic_Party" title="German Social Democratic Party">Social Democrats</a> (SPD) and the <a href="/wiki/German_Communist_Party" title="German Communist Party">Communists</a> (KPD) &#8211; and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> assumed control of the organization. The <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Program" title="National Socialist Program">National Socialist Program</a> or "25 Points" was adopted in 1920 and called for a united <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Germany" title="Greater Germany">Greater Germany</a> that would deny citizenship to <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> or those of Jewish descent, while also supporting land reform and the <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalization</a> of some industries. In <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i> ("My Struggle"; 1924&#8211;1925), Hitler outlined the anti-Semitism and anti-Communism at the heart of his political philosophy, as well as his disdain for <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democracy</a> and his belief in Germany\'s right to territorial expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999243-44,_248-49_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999243-44,_248-49-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Nazi Party won the greatest share of the popular vote in the two <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_(Weimar_Republic)" title="Reichstag (Weimar Republic)">Reichstag</a> general elections of 1932, making them the largest party in the legislature by far, but still short of an outright majority. Because none of the parties were willing or able to put together a coalition government, in 1933 Hitler was appointed <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">Chancellor of Germany</a> by President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_Von_Hindenburg" title="Paul Von Hindenburg">Paul Von Hindenburg</a>, through the support and connivance of traditional conservative nationalists who believed that they could control him and his party. Through the use of emergency presidential decrees by Hindenburg, and a change in the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Constitution" title="Weimar Constitution">Weimar Constitution</a> which allowed the Cabinet to rule by direct decree, bypassing both Hindenburg and the Reichstag, the Nazis had soon established a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party state</a>.\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">Sturmabteilung</a></i> (SA) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">Schutzstaffel</a></i> (SS) functioned as the paramilitary organizations of the Nazi Party. Using the SS for the task, Hitler purged the party\'s more socially and economically radical factions in the mid-1934 <a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Long_Knives" title="Night of the Long Knives">Night of the Long Knives</a>, including the leadership of the SA.  After the death of President Hindenburg, political power was concentrated in Hitler\'s hands and he became Germany\'s head of state as well as the head of the government, with the title of <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="F&#252;hrer">F&#252;hrer</a></i>, meaning "leader". From that point, Hitler was effectively the <a href="/wiki/Dictator" title="Dictator">dictator</a> of Nazi Germany, which was also known as the "Third Reich", under which Jews, political opponents and other "undesirable" elements were marginalized, imprisoned or murdered. Many millions of people were eventually exterminated in a genocide which became known as <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, including around two-thirds of the Jewish population of Europe.\n</p><p>Following Germany\'s defeat in World War II and the discovery of the full extent of the Holocaust, Nazi ideology became universally disgraced. It is widely regarded as immoral and <a href="/wiki/Evil" title="Evil">evil</a>, with only a few fringe <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racist</a> groups, usually referred to as <a href="/wiki/Neo-Nazism" title="Neo-Nazism">neo-Nazis</a>, describing themselves as followers of National Socialism.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" Nazism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_NSDAP_(1920%E2%80%931945).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_the_NSDAP_%281920%E2%80%931945%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_NSDAP_%281920%E2%80%931945%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_the_NSDAP_%281920%E2%80%931945%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_NSDAP_%281920%E2%80%931945%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_the_NSDAP_%281920%E2%80%931945%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_NSDAP_%281920%E2%80%931945%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_NSDAP_(1920%E2%80%931945).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flag of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a>, similar but not identical to the national <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Germany#Nazi_Germany_and_World_War_II_(1933&#8211;1945)" title="Flag of Germany">flag of Nazi Germany</a> (1933&#8211;1945), in which the <a href="/wiki/Swastika" title="Swastika">swastika</a> is slightly off-centred</div></div></div>\n<p>The full name of the party was <i>Nationalsozialistische Deutsche Arbeiterpartei</i> (English: <span lang="en">National-Socialist German Workers\' Party</span>) for which they officially used the acronym NSDAP.\n</p><p>The term "Nazi" was in use before the rise of the NSDAP as a colloquial and derogatory word for a backwards farmer or <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasant</a>, characterizing an awkward and clumsy person. In this sense, the word <i>Nazi</i> was a <a href="/wiki/Hypocorism" title="Hypocorism">hypocorism</a> of the German male name Ignatz (itself a variation of the name <a href="/wiki/Ignatius" title="Ignatius">Ignatius</a>) &#8211; Ignatz being a common name at the time in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, the area from which the NSDAP emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GottliebMorgensen2007_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-GottliebMorgensen2007-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarperOED_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-HarperOED-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1920s, political opponents of the NSDAP in the German <a href="/wiki/Labour_movement" title="Labour movement">labour movement</a> seized on this and &#8211; using the earlier abbreviated term "Sozi" for <i>Sozialist</i> (English: <span lang="en">Socialist</span>) as an example<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> &#8211; shortened the first part of the NSDAP\'s name, <i>[Na]tionalso[zi]alistische</i>, to the dismissive "Nazi", in order to associate them with the derogatory use of the term mentioned above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lepage2009_9_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lepage2009_9-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarperOED_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-HarperOED-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sourcebook_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sourcebook-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DailyTelegraph23102011_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-DailyTelegraph23102011-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seebold2002_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seebold2002-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the NSDAP\'s rise to power in the 1930s, the use of the term "Nazi" by itself or in terms such as "<a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi_regime" title="Nazi regime">Nazi regime</a>" and so on was popularised by German exiles. From them, the term spread into other languages and it was eventually brought back into Germany after World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sourcebook_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sourcebook-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The NSDAP briefly adopted the designation "Nazi"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2017)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> in an attempt to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reappropriate" title="Reappropriate">reappropriate</a> the term, but it soon gave up this effort and generally avoided using the term while it was in power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sourcebook_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sourcebook-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DailyTelegraph23102011_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-DailyTelegraph23102011-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Position_within_the_political_spectrum">Position within the political spectrum</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WWII,_Europe,_Germany,_%22Nazi_Hierarchy,_Hitler,_Goering,_Goebbels,_Hess%22,_The_Desperate_Years_p143_-_NARA_-_196509.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1876" data-file-width="2963" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/WWII%2C_Europe%2C_Germany%2C_%22Nazi_Hierarchy%2C_Hitler%2C_Goering%2C_Goebbels%2C_Hess%22%2C_The_Desperate_Years_p143_-_NARA_-_196509.jpg/220px-WWII%2C_Europe%2C_Germany%2C_%22Nazi_Hierarchy%2C_Hitler%2C_Goering%2C_Goebbels%2C_Hess%22%2C_The_Desperate_Years_p143_-_NARA_-_196509.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/WWII%2C_Europe%2C_Germany%2C_%22Nazi_Hierarchy%2C_Hitler%2C_Goering%2C_Goebbels%2C_Hess%22%2C_The_Desperate_Years_p143_-_NARA_-_196509.jpg/330px-WWII%2C_Europe%2C_Germany%2C_%22Nazi_Hierarchy%2C_Hitler%2C_Goering%2C_Goebbels%2C_Hess%22%2C_The_Desperate_Years_p143_-_NARA_-_196509.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/WWII%2C_Europe%2C_Germany%2C_%22Nazi_Hierarchy%2C_Hitler%2C_Goering%2C_Goebbels%2C_Hess%22%2C_The_Desperate_Years_p143_-_NARA_-_196509.jpg/440px-WWII%2C_Europe%2C_Germany%2C_%22Nazi_Hierarchy%2C_Hitler%2C_Goering%2C_Goebbels%2C_Hess%22%2C_The_Desperate_Years_p143_-_NARA_-_196509.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WWII,_Europe,_Germany,_%22Nazi_Hierarchy,_Hitler,_Goering,_Goebbels,_Hess%22,_The_Desperate_Years_p143_-_NARA_-_196509.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Foreground, left to right: F&#252;hrer <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>; <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann G&#246;ring">Hermann G&#246;ring</a>; Minister of Propaganda <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Joseph Goebbels</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Hess" title="Rudolf Hess">Rudolf Hess</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02134,_Bad_Harzburg,_Gr%C3%BCndung_der_Harzburger_Front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2148" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02134%2C_Bad_Harzburg%2C_Gr%C3%BCndung_der_Harzburger_Front.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02134%2C_Bad_Harzburg%2C_Gr%C3%BCndung_der_Harzburger_Front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02134%2C_Bad_Harzburg%2C_Gr%C3%BCndung_der_Harzburger_Front.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02134%2C_Bad_Harzburg%2C_Gr%C3%BCndung_der_Harzburger_Front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02134%2C_Bad_Harzburg%2C_Gr%C3%BCndung_der_Harzburger_Front.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02134%2C_Bad_Harzburg%2C_Gr%C3%BCndung_der_Harzburger_Front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-02134,_Bad_Harzburg,_Gr%C3%BCndung_der_Harzburger_Front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nazis alongside members of the far-right <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monarchism" title="Monarchism">monarchist</a> <a href="/wiki/German_National_People%27s_Party" title="German National People\'s Party">German National People\'s Party</a> (DNVP) during the brief NSDAP&#8211;DNVP alliance in the <a href="/wiki/Harzburg_Front" title="Harzburg Front">Harzburg Front</a> from 1931 to 1932</div></div></div>\n<p>The majority of scholars identify Nazism in both theory and practice as a form of <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right politics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fritzsche_Eatwell_Griffin_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fritzsche_Eatwell_Griffin-14">[14]</a></sup> Far-right themes in Nazism include the argument that superior people have a right to dominate other people and purge society of supposed inferior elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oliver_H._Woshinsky_2008._p._156_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oliver_H._Woshinsky_2008._p._156-15">[15]</a></sup> Adolf Hitler and other proponents denied that Nazism was either left-wing or right-wing, instead they officially portrayed Nazism as a <a href="/wiki/Syncretic_politics" title="Syncretic politics">syncretic</a> movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adolf_Hitler_p._170_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adolf_Hitler_p._170-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudy_Koshar_1986._p._190_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudy_Koshar_1986._p._190-17">[17]</a></sup> In <i>Mein Kampf</i>, Hitler directly attacked both left-wing and right-wing politics in Germany, saying:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Today our left-wing politicians in particular are constantly insisting that their craven-hearted and obsequious foreign policy necessarily results from the disarmament of Germany, whereas the truth is that this is the policy of traitors ... But the politicians of the Right deserve exactly the same reproach. It was through their miserable cowardice that those ruffians of Jews who came into power in 1918 were able to rob the nation of its arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adolf_Hitler_2010._p._287_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adolf_Hitler_2010._p._287-18">[18]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>In a speech given in Munich on 12 April 1922, Hitler stated that:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>There are only two possibilities in Germany; do not imagine that the people will forever go with the middle party, the party of compromises; one day it will turn to those who have most consistently foretold the coming ruin and have sought to dissociate themselves from it. And that party is either the Left: and then God help us! for it will lead us to complete destruction - to Bolshevism, or else it is a party of the Right which at the last, when the people is in utter despair, when it has lost all its spirit and has no longer any faith in anything, is determined for its part ruthlessly to seize the reins of power - that is the beginning of resistance of which I spoke a few minutes ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holocaustreader_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-holocaustreader-19">[19]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>When asked<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2017)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> whether he supported the "bourgeois right-wing", Hitler claimed that Nazism was not exclusively for any class and he indicated that it favoured neither the left nor the right, but preserved "pure" elements from both "camps" by stating: "From the camp of bourgeois <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a>, it takes national resolve, and from the <a href="/wiki/Dialectical_materialism" title="Dialectical materialism">materialism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> dogma, living, creative Socialism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-commentary_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-commentary-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historians regard the equation of National Socialism as \'Hitlerism\' as too simplistic since the term was used prior to the rise of Hitler and the Nazis and the different ideologies incorporated into Nazism were already well established in certain parts of German society before <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999135_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999135-21">[21]</a></sup> The Nazis were strongly influenced by the post&#8211;World War I far-right in Germany, which held common beliefs such as anti-Marxism, anti-liberalism and antisemitism, along with <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, contempt for the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> and condemnation of the Weimar Republic for signing the armistice in November 1918 which later led it to sign the Treaty of Versailles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peukert,_Detlev_1993_pp._73-74_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peukert,_Detlev_1993_pp._73-74-22">[22]</a></sup> A major inspiration for the Nazis were the far-right nationalist <i><a href="/wiki/Freikorps#Post-World_War_I" title="Freikorps">Freikorps</a></i>, paramilitary organizations that engaged in political violence after World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peukert,_Detlev_1993_pp._73-74_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peukert,_Detlev_1993_pp._73-74-22">[22]</a></sup> Initially, the post&#8211;World War I German far-right was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Monarchism" title="Monarchism">monarchists</a>, but the younger generation, which was associated with <i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalism, was more radical and it did not express any emphasis on the restoration of the German monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peukert,_Detlev_1993_p._74_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peukert,_Detlev_1993_p._74-23">[23]</a></sup> This younger generation desired to dismantle the Weimar Republic and create a new radical and strong state based upon a martial ruling ethic that could revive the "Spirit of 1914" which was associated with German national unity (<i><a href="/wiki/Volksgemeinschaft" title="Volksgemeinschaft">Volksgemeinschaft</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peukert,_Detlev_1993_p._74_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peukert,_Detlev_1993_p._74-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Nazis, the far-right monarchists, the <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> <a href="/wiki/German_National_People%27s_Party" title="German National People\'s Party">German National People\'s Party</a> (DNVP) and others, such as monarchist officers in the German Army and several prominent industrialists, formed an alliance in opposition to the Weimar Republic on 11 October 1931 in <a href="/wiki/Bad_Harzburg" title="Bad Harzburg">Bad Harzburg</a>, officially known as the "National Front", but commonly referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Harzburg_Front" title="Harzburg Front">Harzburg Front</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-machtergreifung_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-machtergreifung-24">[24]</a></sup> The Nazis stated that the alliance was purely tactical and they continued to have differences with the DNVP. The Nazis described the DNVP as a bourgeois party and they called themselves an anti-bourgeois party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-machtergreifung_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-machtergreifung-24">[24]</a></sup> After the elections of July 1932, the alliance broke down when the DNVP lost many of its seats in the Reichstag. The Nazis denounced them as "an insignificant heap of reactionaries".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-machtergreifung5_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-machtergreifung5-25">[25]</a></sup> The DNVP responded by denouncing the Nazis for their socialism, their street violence and the "economic experiments" that would take place if the Nazis ever rose to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-machtergreifung6_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-machtergreifung6-26">[26]</a></sup> But amidst an inconclusive political situation in which conservative politicians <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Papen" title="Franz von Papen">Franz von Papen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurt_von_Schleicher" title="Kurt von Schleicher">Kurt von Schleicher</a> were unable to form stable governments without the Nazis, Papen proposed to President Hindenburg to appoint Hitler as Chancellor at the head of a government formed primarily of conservatives, with only three Nazi ministers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky1985104-106_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky1985104-106-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Hindenburg did so, and contrary to the expectations of Papen and the DNVP, Hitler was soon able to establish a Nazi one-party dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky1985106-107_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky1985106-107-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kaiser" title="Kaiser">Kaiser</a> <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Wilhelm II</a>, who was pressured to abdicate the throne and flee into exile amidst an attempted communist revolution in Germany, initially supported the Nazi Party. His four sons, including Prince <a href="/wiki/Prince_Eitel_Friedrich_of_Prussia" title="Prince Eitel Friedrich of Prussia">Eitel Friedrich</a> and Prince <a href="/wiki/Prince_Oskar_of_Prussia" title="Prince Oskar of Prussia">Oskar</a>, became members of the Nazi Party in hopes that in exchange for their support, the Nazis would permit the restoration of the monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicholas_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nicholas-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There were factions within the Nazi Party, both conservative and radical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael_Mann_2004._p._183_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Mann_2004._p._183-31">[31]</a></sup> The conservative Nazi <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann G&#246;ring">Hermann G&#246;ring</a> urged Hitler to conciliate with <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionaries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael_Mann_2004._p._183_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Mann_2004._p._183-31">[31]</a></sup> Other prominent conservative Nazis included <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reinhard_Heydrich" title="Reinhard Heydrich">Reinhard Heydrich</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foundations_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-foundations-32">[32]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the radical Nazi <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Joseph Goebbels</a> opposed capitalism, viewing it as having Jews at its core and he stressed the need for the party to emphasize both a <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletarian</a> and a national character. Those views were shared by <a href="/wiki/Otto_Strasser" title="Otto Strasser">Otto Strasser</a>, who later left the Nazi Party in the belief that Hitler had allegedly betrayed the party\'s socialist goals by endorsing capitalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael_Mann_2004._p._183_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Mann_2004._p._183-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the Nazi Party emerged from obscurity to become a major political force after 1929, the conservative faction rapidly gained more influence, as wealthy donors took an interest in the Nazis as a potential bulwark against communism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The Nazi Party had previously been financed almost entirely from membership dues, but after 1929 its leadership began actively seeking donations from German industrialists, and Hitler began holding dozens of fundraising meetings with business leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In the midst of the Great Depression, facing the possibility of economic ruin on the one hand and a Communist or Social Democratic government on the other hand, German business increasingly turned to Nazism as offering a way out of the situation, by promising a state-driven economy that would support, rather than attack, existing business interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> By January 1933, the Nazi Party had secured the support of important sectors of German industry, mainly among the steel and coal producers, the insurance business and the chemical industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Large segments of the Nazi Party, particularly among the members of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">Sturmabteilung</a></i> (SA), were committed to the party\'s official socialist, revolutionary and <a href="/wiki/Anti-capitalism" title="Anti-capitalism">anti-capitalist</a> positions and expected both a social and an economic revolution when the party gained power in 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joseph_W._Bendersky_2007._p._96_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joseph_W._Bendersky_2007._p._96-37">[37]</a></sup> In the period immediately before the Nazi seizure of power, there were even <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democrats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">Communists</a> who switched sides and became known as "<a href="/wiki/Beefsteak_Nazi" title="Beefsteak Nazi">Beefsteak Nazis</a>": brown on the outside and red inside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The leader of the SA, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_R%C3%B6hm" title="Ernst R&#246;hm">Ernst R&#246;hm</a>, pushed for a "second revolution" (the "first revolution" being the Nazis\' seizure of power) that would enact socialist policies. Furthermore, R&#246;hm desired that the SA absorb the much smaller German Army into its ranks under his leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joseph_W._Bendersky_2007._p._96_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Joseph_W._Bendersky_2007._p._96-37">[37]</a></sup> Once the Nazis achieved power, R&#246;hm\'s SA was directed by Hitler to violently suppress the parties of the left, but they also began attacks against individuals deemed to be associated with conservative reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967123-124;130_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967123-124;130-39">[39]</a></sup> Hitler saw R&#246;hm\'s independent actions as violating and possibly threatening his leadership, as well as jeopardising the regime by alienating the conservative President <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a> and the conservative-oriented German Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nyomarkay1967_133_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nyomarkay1967_133-40">[40]</a></sup> This resulted in Hitler purging R&#246;hm and other radical members of the SA in 1934, in what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Long_Knives" title="Night of the Long Knives">Night of the Long Knives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nyomarkay1967_133_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nyomarkay1967_133-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before he joined the Bavarian Army to fight in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Hitler had lived a <a href="/wiki/Bohemianism" title="Bohemianism">bohemian</a> lifestyle as a petty street watercolour artist in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> and he maintained elements of this lifestyle later on, going to bed very late and rising in the afternoon, even after he became Chancellor and then F&#252;hrer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publishers_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-publishers-41">[41]</a></sup> After the war, his battalion was absorbed by the <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_Soviet_Republic" title="Bavarian Soviet Republic">Bavarian Soviet Republic</a> from 1918 to 1919, where he was elected Deputy Battalion Representative. According to historian Thomas Weber, Hitler attended the funeral of communist <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Eisner" title="Kurt Eisner">Kurt Eisner</a> (a German Jew), wearing a black mourning armband on one arm and a red communist armband on the other,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_Weber_2011._p._251_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_Weber_2011._p._251-42">[42]</a></sup> which he took as evidence that Hitler\'s political beliefs had not yet solidified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_Weber_2011._p._251_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_Weber_2011._p._251-42">[42]</a></sup> In <i>Mein Kampf</i>, Hitler never mentioned any service with the Bavarian Soviet Republic and he stated that he became an antisemite in 1913 during his years in Vienna. This statement has been disputed by the contention that he was not an antisemite at that time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_S._Gaab_2008._p._61_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey_S._Gaab_2008._p._61-43">[43]</a></sup> even though it is well established that he read many antisemitic tracts and journals during time and admired <a href="/wiki/Karl_Lueger" title="Karl Lueger">Karl Lueger</a>, the antisemitic mayor of Vienna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw199934-35,_50-52,_60-67_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw199934-35,_50-52,_60-67-44">[44]</a></sup> Hitler altered his political views in response to the signing of the Treaty of Versailles in June 1919 and it was then that he became an antisemitic, German nationalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffrey_S._Gaab_2008._p._61_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffrey_S._Gaab_2008._p._61-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hitler expressed opposition to capitalism, regarding it as having Jewish origins and accusing capitalism of holding nations ransom to the interests of a parasitic <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitanism" title="Cosmopolitanism">cosmopolitan</a> <a href="/wiki/Rentier_capitalism" title="Rentier capitalism">rentier</a> class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._pp._399-403_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._pp._399-403-45">[45]</a></sup> He also expressed opposition to communism and egalitarian forms of socialism, arguing that inequality and hierarchy are beneficial to the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198549_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198549-46">[46]</a></sup> He believed that communism was invented by the Jews to weaken nations by promoting class struggle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198550_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198550-47">[47]</a></sup> After his rise to power, Hitler took a pragmatic position on economics, accepting private property and allowing capitalist private enterprises to exist so long as they adhered to the goals of the Nazi state, but not tolerating enterprises that he saw as being opposed to the national interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael_Mann_2004._p._183_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Michael_Mann_2004._p._183-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>German business leaders disliked Nazi ideology but came to support Hitler, because they saw the Nazis as a useful ally to promote their interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze2006101_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze2006101-48">[48]</a></sup> Business groups made significant financial contributions to the Nazi Party both before and after the Nazi seizure of power, in the hope that a Nazi dictatorship would eliminate the organized labour movement and the left-wing parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze2006100-101_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze2006100-101-49">[49]</a></sup> Hitler actively sought to gain the support of business leaders by arguing that private enterprise is incompatible with democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze200699_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze200699-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although he opposed communist ideology, Hitler publicly praised the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>\'s leader <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stalinism" title="Stalinism">Stalinism</a> on numerous occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fran&#231;ois_Furet_1999._pp._191-192_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fran&#231;ois_Furet_1999._pp._191-192-51">[51]</a></sup> Hitler commended Stalin for seeking to purify the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Communist Party of the Soviet Union</a> of Jewish influences, noting Stalin\'s purging of Jewish communists such as <a href="/wiki/Leon_Trotsky" title="Leon Trotsky">Leon Trotsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grigory_Zinoviev" title="Grigory Zinoviev">Grigory Zinoviev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lev_Kamenev" title="Lev Kamenev">Lev Kamenev</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Radek" title="Karl Radek">Karl Radek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-communism_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-communism-52">[52]</a></sup> While Hitler had always intended to bring Germany into conflict with the Soviet Union so he could gain <i><a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a></i> ("living space"), he supported a temporary strategic alliance between Nazi Germany and the Soviet Union to form a common anti-liberal front so they could defeat liberal democracies, particularly <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fran&#231;ois_Furet_1999._pp._191-192_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fran&#231;ois_Furet_1999._pp._191-192-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_timeline_of_Nazism" title="Early timeline of Nazism">Early timeline of Nazism</a></div>\n<h3><span id="V.C3.B6lkisch_nationalism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="V&#246;lkisch_nationalism">V&#246;lkisch nationalism</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pan-Germanism" title="Pan-Germanism">Pan-Germanism</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism" title="German nationalism">German nationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_Question" title="German Question">German Question</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">Unification of Germany</a>, and <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkisch_movement" title="V&#246;lkisch movement">V&#246;lkisch movement</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="286" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte.jpg/170px-Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte.jpg/255px-Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8d/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte" title="Johann Gottlieb Fichte">Johann Gottlieb Fichte</a>, considered one of the fathers of German nationalism</div></div></div>\n<p>One of the most significant ideological influences on the Nazis was the German nationalist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte" title="Johann Gottlieb Fichte">Johann Gottlieb Fichte</a>, whose works had served as an inspiration to Hitler and other Nazi Party members, including <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Eckart" title="Dietrich Eckart">Dietrich Eckart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Fanck" title="Arnold Fanck">Arnold Fanck</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryback2010_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ryback2010-53">[53]</a></sup> In <i>Speeches to the German Nation</i> (1808), written amid <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleonic</a> France\'s occupation of Berlin, Fichte called for a German national revolution against the French occupiers, making passionate public speeches, arming his students for battle against the French and stressing the need for action by the German nation so it could free itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryback2010_129_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ryback2010_129-54">[54]</a></sup> Fichte\'s nationalism was populist and opposed to traditional elites, spoke of the need for a "People\'s War" (<i>Volkskrieg</i>) and put forth concepts similar to those which the Nazis adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryback2010_129_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ryback2010_129-54">[54]</a></sup> Fichte promoted German <a href="/wiki/Exceptionalism" title="Exceptionalism">exceptionalism</a> and stressed the need for the German nation to purify itself (including purging the German language of French words, a policy that the Nazis undertook upon their rise to power).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryback2010_129_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ryback2010_129-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another important figure in pre-Nazi <i>v&#246;lkisch</i> thinking was <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Heinrich_Riehl" title="Wilhelm Heinrich Riehl">Wilhelm Heinrich Riehl</a>, whose work&#8212;<i>Land und Leute</i> (<i>Land and People</i>, written between 1857 and 1863)&#8212;collectively tied the organic German Volk to its native landscape and nature, a pairing which stood in stark opposition to the mechanical and materialistic civilization which was then developing as a result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">industrialization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Geographers <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Ratzel" title="Friedrich Ratzel">Friedrich Ratzel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Haushofer" title="Karl Haushofer">Karl Haushofer</a> borrowed from Riehl\'s work as did Nazi ideologues Alfred Rosenberg and Paul Schultze-Naumburg, both of whom employed some of Riehl\'s philosophy in arguing that "each nation-state was an organism that required a particular living space in order to survive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Riehl\'s influence is overtly discernible in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blut_und_Boden" title="Blut und Boden">Blut und Boden</a></i> (<i>Blood and Soil</i>) philosophy introduced by <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Spengler" title="Oswald Spengler">Oswald Spengler</a>, which the Nazi agriculturalist Walther Darr&#233; and other prominent Nazis adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalism denounced soulless <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secularization" title="Secularization">secularised</a> <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban</a> industrial society, while advocating a "superior" society based on ethnic German "folk" culture and German "blood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia7_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia7-59">[59]</a></sup> It denounced foreigners and foreign ideas and declared that Jews, <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasons</a> and others were "traitors to the nation" and unworthy of inclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-constructing_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-constructing-60">[60]</a></sup> <i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalism saw the world in terms of <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romanticism</a> and it viewed societies as organic, extolling the virtues of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> life, condemning the neglect of tradition and the decay of morals, denounced the destruction of the natural environment and condemned "cosmopolitan" cultures such as Jews and Romani.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Olsen_1999._p._62_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Olsen_1999._p._62-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first party that attempted to combine nationalism and socialism was the <a href="/wiki/German_Workers%27_Party_(Austria-Hungary)" title="German Workers\' Party (Austria-Hungary)">(Austria-Hungary) German Workers\' Party</a>, which predominantly aimed to solve the conflict between the Austrian Germans and the Czechs in the multi-ethnic <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian Empire</a>, then part of <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In 1896 the German politician Friedrich Naumann formed the National-Social Association which aimed to combine German nationalism and a non-Marxist form of socialism together; the attempt turned out to be futile and the idea of linking nationalism with socialism quickly became equated with antisemites, extreme German nationalists and the <i>V&#246;lkisch</i> movement in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999135_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999135-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VonSchoenerer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="717" data-file-width="506" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/VonSchoenerer.jpg/170px-VonSchoenerer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/VonSchoenerer.jpg/255px-VonSchoenerer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/VonSchoenerer.jpg/340px-VonSchoenerer.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VonSchoenerer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Georg_Ritter_von_Sch%C3%B6nerer" title="Georg Ritter von Sch&#246;nerer">Georg Ritter von Sch&#246;nerer</a>, a major exponent of Pan-Germanism</div></div></div>\n<p>During the era of <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Imperial Germany</a>, <i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalism was overshadowed by both Prussian patriotism and the federalist tradition of its various component states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nina_Witoszek_2002._pp._89-90_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nina_Witoszek_2002._pp._89-90-63">[63]</a></sup> The events of World War I, including the end of the Prussian monarchy in Germany, resulted in a surge of revolutionary <i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witoszek_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-witoszek-64">[64]</a></sup> The Nazis supported such revolutionary <i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalist policies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nina_Witoszek_2002._pp._89-90_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nina_Witoszek_2002._pp._89-90-63">[63]</a></sup> and they claimed that their ideology was influenced by the leadership and policies of <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">German Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerwarth2007_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerwarth2007-65">[65]</a></sup> The Nazis declared that they were dedicated to continuing the process of creating a unified German <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a> that Bismarck had begun and desired to achieve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerwarth2007_149_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerwarth2007_149-66">[66]</a></sup> While Hitler was supportive of Bismarck\'s creation of the German Empire, he was critical of Bismarck\'s moderate domestic policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGerwarth200754_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGerwarth200754-67">[67]</a></sup> On the issue of Bismarck\'s support of a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kleindeutschland" title="Kleindeutschland">Kleindeutschland</a></i> ("Lesser Germany", excluding Austria) versus the Pan-German <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_question#Later_influence" title="German question">Gro&#223;deutschland</a></i> ("Greater Germany") which the Nazis advocated, Hitler stated that Bismarck\'s attainment of <i>Kleindeutschland</i> was the "highest achievement" Bismarck could have achieved "within the limits possible at that time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerwarth2007_131_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerwarth2007_131-68">[68]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i> (<i>My Struggle</i>), Hitler presented himself as a "second Bismarck".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerwarth2007_131_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gerwarth2007_131-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During his youth in Austria, Hitler was politically influenced by Austrian Pan-Germanist proponent <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ritter_von_Sch%C3%B6nerer" title="Georg Ritter von Sch&#246;nerer">Georg Ritter von Sch&#246;nerer</a>, who advocated radical <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism_in_Austria" title="German nationalism in Austria">German nationalism</a>, antisemitism, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Catholicism" title="Anti-Catholicism">anti-Catholicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavic_sentiment" title="Anti-Slavic sentiment">anti-Slavic sentiment</a> and anti-Habsburg views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicholls236237_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-nicholls236237-69">[69]</a></sup> From von Sch&#246;nerer and his followers, Hitler adopted for the Nazi movement the <i>Heil</i> greeting, the <i>F&#252;hrer</i> title and the model of absolute party leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicholls236237_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-nicholls236237-69">[69]</a></sup> Hitler was also impressed by the <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populist</a> antisemitism and the anti-liberal bourgeois agitation of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Lueger" title="Karl Lueger">Karl Lueger</a>, who as the mayor of Vienna during Hitler\'s time in the city used a rabble-rousing style of oratory that appealed to the wider masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicholls159160_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-nicholls159160-70">[70]</a></sup> Unlike von Sch&#246;nerer, Lueger was not a German nationalist and instead was a pro-Catholic Habsburg supporter and only used German nationalist notions occasionally for his own agenda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nicholls159160_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-nicholls159160-70">[70]</a></sup> Although Hitler praised both Lueger and Sch&#246;nerer, he criticized the former for not applying a racial doctrine against the Jews and Slavs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racial_theories_and_antisemitism">Racial theories and antisemitism</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nazism_and_race" title="Nazism and race">Nazism and race</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_de_Gobineau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="659" data-file-width="518" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Arthur_de_Gobineau.jpg/200px-Arthur_de_Gobineau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Arthur_de_Gobineau.jpg/300px-Arthur_de_Gobineau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Arthur_de_Gobineau.jpg/400px-Arthur_de_Gobineau.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arthur_de_Gobineau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arthur_de_Gobineau" title="Arthur de Gobineau">Arthur de Gobineau</a>, one of the key inventors of the theory of the "<a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan race</a>"</div></div></div>\n<p>The concept of the <a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan race</a>, which the Nazis promoted, stems from racial theories asserting that Europeans are the descendants of Indo-Iranian settlers, people of ancient <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and ancient <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-72">[72]</a></sup> Proponents of this theory based their assertion on the fact that words in European languages and words in Indo-Iranian languages have similar pronunciations and meanings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Herder" title="Johann Gottfried Herder">Johann Gottfried Herder</a> argued that the Germanic peoples held close racial connections to the ancient Indians and the ancient Persians, who he claimed were advanced peoples that possessed a great capacity for wisdom, nobility, restraint and science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-72">[72]</a></sup> Contemporaries of Herder used the concept of the Aryan race to draw a distinction between what they deemed to be "high and noble" Aryan culture versus that of "parasitic" Semitic culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_72-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Notions of <a href="/wiki/White_supremacy" title="White supremacy">white supremacy</a> and Aryan racial superiority were combined in the 19th century, with white supremacists maintaining the belief that certain groups of <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white people</a> were members of an Aryan "master race" that is superior to other races and particularly superior to the Semitic race, which they associated with "cultural sterility".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_72-4"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_de_Gobineau" title="Arthur de Gobineau">Arthur de Gobineau</a>, a French racial theorist and aristocrat, blamed the fall of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancien_r%C3%A9gime" title="Ancien r&#233;gime">ancien r&#233;gime</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> on racial degeneracy caused by <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">racial intermixing</a>, which he argued had destroyed the purity of the Aryan race, a term which he only reserved for Germanic people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Gobineau\'s theories, which attracted a strong following in Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-73">[73]</a></sup> emphasized the existence of an irreconcilable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarity_in_international_relations" title="Polarity in international relations">polarity</a> between Aryan (<a href="/wiki/Germanic_culture" title="Germanic culture">Germanic</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_culture" title="Jewish culture">Jewish cultures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_72-5"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1600-06,_Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="367" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1600-06%2C_Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1600-06%2C_Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1600-06%2C_Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1600-06%2C_Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1600-06%2C_Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1600-06%2C_Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_119-1600-06,_Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain" title="Houston Stewart Chamberlain">Houston Stewart Chamberlain</a>, whose book <i>The Foundations of the Nineteenth Century</i> would prove to be a seminal work in the history of German nationalism</div></div></div>\n<p>Aryan <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mysticism</a> claimed that <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> originated in Aryan religious traditions, and that Jews had usurped the legend from Aryans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_72-6"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Houston_Stewart_Chamberlain" title="Houston Stewart Chamberlain">Houston Stewart Chamberlain</a>, an English-born German proponent of racial theory, supported notions of Germanic supremacy and antisemitism in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-73">[73]</a></sup> Chamberlain\'s work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Foundations_of_the_Nineteenth_Century" title="The Foundations of the Nineteenth Century">The Foundations of the Nineteenth Century</a></i> (1899), praised Germanic peoples for their creativity and idealism while asserting that the Germanic spirit was threatened by a "Jewish" spirit of selfishness and <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-73">[73]</a></sup> Chamberlain used his thesis to promote <a href="/wiki/Monarchism" title="Monarchism">monarchical</a> <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatism</a> while denouncing <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-73">[73]</a></sup> The book became popular, especially in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_73-5"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-73">[73]</a></sup> Chamberlain stressed a nation\'s need to maintain its racial purity in order to prevent its degeneration and argued that racial intermingling with Jews should never be permitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_73-6"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-73">[73]</a></sup> In 1923, Chamberlain met Hitler, whom he admired as a leader of the rebirth of the free spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia9_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia9-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Madison_Grant" title="Madison Grant">Madison Grant</a>\'s work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Passing_of_the_Great_Race" title="The Passing of the Great Race">The Passing of the Great Race</a></i> (1916) advocated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nordicism" title="Nordicism">Nordicism</a> and proposed that a <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a> program should be implemented in order to preserve the purity of the Nordic race. After reading the book, Hitler called it "my Bible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Germany, the belief that Jews were economically exploiting Germans became prominent due to the ascendancy of many wealthy Jews into prominent positions upon the <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Germany" title="Unification of Germany">unification of Germany</a> in 1871.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brustein207_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brustein207-77">[77]</a></sup> From 1871 to the early 20th century, German Jews were overrepresented in Germany\'s upper and middle classes while they were underrepresented in Germany\'s lower classes, particularly in the fields of agricultural and industrial labour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brustein210_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brustein210-78">[78]</a></sup> German Jewish financiers and bankers played a key role in fostering Germany\'s economic growth from 1871 to 1913 and they benefited enormously from this boom. In 1908, amongst the twenty-nine wealthiest German families with aggregate fortunes of up to 55 million marks at the time, five were Jewish and the <a href="/wiki/Rothschild_family" title="Rothschild family">Rothschilds</a> were the second wealthiest German family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The predominance of Jews in Germany\'s banking, commerce and industry sectors during this time period was very high, even though Jews were estimated to account for only 1% of the population of Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brustein207_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brustein207-77">[77]</a></sup> The overrepresentation of Jews in these areas fueled resentment among non-Jewish Germans during periods of economic crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brustein210_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brustein210-78">[78]</a></sup> The 1873 stock market crash and the ensuing depression resulted in a spate of attacks on alleged Jewish economic dominance in Germany and antisemitism increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brustein210_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brustein210-78">[78]</a></sup> During this time period, in the 1870s, German <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkisch_movement" title="V&#246;lkisch movement"><i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalism</a> began to adopt antisemitic and racist themes and it was also adopted by a number of radical right political movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-witoszek10_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-witoszek10-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Radical Antisemitism was promoted by prominent advocates of <i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalism, including <a href="/wiki/Eugen_Diederichs" title="Eugen Diederichs">Eugen Diederichs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_de_Lagarde" title="Paul de Lagarde">Paul de Lagarde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Julius_Langbehn" title="Julius Langbehn">Julius Langbehn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Olsen_1999._p._62_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Olsen_1999._p._62-61">[61]</a></sup> De Lagarde called the Jews a "<a href="/wiki/Bacillus" title="Bacillus">bacillus</a>, the carriers of decay ... who pollute every national culture ... and destroy all faiths with their materialistic liberalism" and he called for the extermination of the Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jack_Fischel_1998._p._5_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jack_Fischel_1998._p._5-81">[81]</a></sup> Langbehn called for a war of annihilation against the Jews, and his genocidal policies were later published by the Nazis and given to soldiers on the front during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jack_Fischel_1998._p._5_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jack_Fischel_1998._p._5-81">[81]</a></sup> One antisemitic ideologue of the period, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Lange_(journalist)" title="Friedrich Lange (journalist)">Friedrich Lange</a>, even used the term "National Socialism" to describe his own anti-capitalist take on the <i>V&#246;lkisch</i> nationalist template.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottlieb_Fichte" title="Johann Gottlieb Fichte">Johann Gottlieb Fichte</a> accused <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> in Germany of having been and inevitably of continuing to be a "state within a state" that threatened German national unity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryback2010_129_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ryback2010_129-54">[54]</a></sup> Fichte promoted two options in order to address this, his first one being the creation of a Jewish state in <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> so the Jews could be impelled to leave Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyback2010130_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyback2010130-83">[83]</a></sup> His second option was violence against Jews and he said that the goal of the violence would be "to cut off all their heads in one night, and set new ones on their shoulders, which should not contain a single Jewish idea".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyback2010130_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyback2010130-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1.2_The_Jewish_Bolshevism_Image.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1324" data-file-width="1025" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/1.2_The_Jewish_Bolshevism_Image.png/170px-1.2_The_Jewish_Bolshevism_Image.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/1.2_The_Jewish_Bolshevism_Image.png/255px-1.2_The_Jewish_Bolshevism_Image.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/1.2_The_Jewish_Bolshevism_Image.png/340px-1.2_The_Jewish_Bolshevism_Image.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1.2_The_Jewish_Bolshevism_Image.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caricatures of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> leaders <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Radek" title="Karl Radek">Karl Radek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Martov" title="Julius Martov">Julius Martov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emma_Goldman" title="Emma Goldman">Emma Goldman</a> from <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rosenberg" title="Alfred Rosenberg">Alfred Rosenberg</a>\'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Jewish_Bolshevism" title="The Jewish Bolshevism">The Jewish Bolshevism</a></i>, which assert that Bolshevism is a Jewish ideology</div></div></div>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Protocols_of_the_Elders_of_Zion" title="The Protocols of the Elders of Zion">The Protocols of the Elders of Zion</a></i> (1912) is an antisemitic forgery created by the secret service of the Russian Empire, the <a href="/wiki/Okhrana" title="Okhrana">Okhrana</a>. Many antisemites believed it was real and thus it became widely popular after World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stackelberg_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-stackelberg-84">[84]</a></sup> <i>The Protocols</i> claimed that there was a secret international Jewish conspiracy to take over the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kershaw_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-kershaw-85">[85]</a></sup> Hitler had been introduced to <i>The Protocols</i> by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rosenberg" title="Alfred Rosenberg">Alfred Rosenberg</a> and from 1920 onwards he focused his attacks by claiming that <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> and Marxism were directly connected, that Jews and <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a> were one and the same and that Marxism was a Jewish ideology-this became known as "<a href="/wiki/Jewish_Bolshevism" title="Jewish Bolshevism">Jewish Bolshevism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dictator_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-dictator-86">[86]</a></sup> Hitler believed that <i>The Protocols</i> were authentic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dictator11_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-dictator11-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prior to the Nazi ascension to power, Hitler often blamed moral degradation on <i><a href="/wiki/Rassenschande" title="Rassenschande">Rassenschande</a></i> ("racial defilement"), a way to assure his followers of his continuing antisemitism, which had been toned down for popular consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koonz2005_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koonz2005-88">[88]</a></sup> Prior to the induction of the Nuremberg Race Laws in 1935 by the Nazis, many German nationalists such as <a href="/wiki/Roland_Freisler" title="Roland Freisler">Roland Freisler</a> strongly supported laws to ban <i>Rassenschande</i> between Aryans and Jews as racial treason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koonz2005_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koonz2005-88">[88]</a></sup> Even before the laws were officially passed, the Nazis banned sexual relations and marriages between party members and Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weikart2009_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weikart2009-89">[89]</a></sup> Party members found guilty of <i>Rassenschande</i> were severely punished; some party members were even sentenced to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon1984_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon1984-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Nazis claimed that Bismarck was unable to complete German national unification because Jews had infiltrated the German parliament and they claimed that their abolition of parliament had ended this obstacle to unification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerwarth2007_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gerwarth2007-65">[65]</a></sup> Using the <a href="/wiki/Stab-in-the-back_myth" title="Stab-in-the-back myth">stab-in-the-back myth</a>, the Nazis accused Jews&#8212;and other populations who it considered non-German&#8212;of possessing extra-national loyalties, thereby exacerbating German <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitism</a> about the <i><a href="/wiki/Jewish_question" title="Jewish question">Judenfrage</a></i> (the Jewish Question), the <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right</a> political <a href="/wiki/Antisemitic_canard" title="Antisemitic canard">canard</a> which was popular when the ethnic <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkisch_movement" title="V&#246;lkisch movement">V&#246;lkisch movement</a> and its politics of <a href="/wiki/Romantic_nationalism" title="Romantic nationalism">Romantic nationalism</a> for establishing a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_question#Later_influence" title="German question">Gro&#223;deutschland</a></i> was strong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PostWWIAntisemitism_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-PostWWIAntisemitism-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JFrage_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-JFrage-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nazism\'s racial policy positions may have developed from the views of important biologists of the 19th century, including French <a href="/wiki/Biologist" title="Biologist">biologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lamarck" title="Jean-Baptiste Lamarck">Jean-Baptiste Lamarck</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a>\'s idealist version of <a href="/wiki/Lamarckism" title="Lamarckism">Lamarckism</a> and the father of <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, German <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botanist</a> <a href="/wiki/Gregor_Mendel" title="Gregor Mendel">Gregor Mendel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_J._Bowler_1989._pp._304-305_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_J._Bowler_1989._pp._304-305-93">[93]</a></sup> However, Haeckel\'s works were later condemned and banned from bookshops and libraries by the Nazis as inappropriate for "National-Socialist formation and education in the Third Reich". This may have been because of his "<a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monist</a>" <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialist</a> philosophy, which the Nazis disliked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robert_J._Richards_2008._pp._7-8_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_J._Richards_2008._pp._7-8-94">[94]</a></sup> Unlike Darwinian theory, Lamarckian theory officially ranked races in a hierarchy of evolution from <a href="/wiki/Ape" title="Ape">apes</a> while Darwinian theory did not grade races in a hierarchy of higher or lower evolution from apes, but simply stated that all humans as a whole had progressed in their evolution from apes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_J._Bowler_1989._pp._304-305_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_J._Bowler_1989._pp._304-305-93">[93]</a></sup> Many Lamarckians viewed "lower" races as having been exposed to debilitating conditions for too long for any significant "improvement" of their condition to take place in the near future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evolution_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-evolution-95">[95]</a></sup> Haeckel utilised Lamarckian theory to describe the existence of interracial struggle and put races on a hierarchy of evolution, ranging from wholly human to <a href="/wiki/Untermensch" title="Untermensch">subhuman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_J._Bowler_1989._pp._304-305_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_J._Bowler_1989._pp._304-305-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">Mendelian inheritance</a>, or Mendelism, was supported by the Nazis, as well as by mainstream eugenicists of the time. The Mendelian theory of inheritance declared that genetic traits and attributes were passed from one generation to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-university14_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-university14-96">[96]</a></sup> Eugenicists used Mendelian inheritance theory to demonstrate the transfer of biological illness and impairments from parents to children, including mental disability, whereas others also utilised Mendelian theory to demonstrate the inheritance of social traits, with racialists claiming a racial nature behind certain general traits such as inventiveness or criminal behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-friedlander_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-friedlander-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Response_to_World_War_I_and_Italian_Fascism">Response to World War I and Italian Fascism</span></h3>\n<p>During World War I, German sociologist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Plenge" title="Johann Plenge">Johann Plenge</a> spoke of the rise of a "National Socialism" in Germany within what he termed the "<a href="/wiki/Spirit_of_1914" title="Spirit of 1914">ideas of 1914</a>" that were a declaration of war against the "ideas of 1789" (the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Kitchen_2006._p._205_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Kitchen_2006._p._205-98">[98]</a></sup> According to Plenge, the "ideas of 1789" which included the rights of man, democracy, individualism and liberalism were being rejected in favour of "the ideas of 1914" which included the "German values" of duty, discipline, law and order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Kitchen_2006._p._205_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Kitchen_2006._p._205-98">[98]</a></sup> Plenge believed that ethnic solidarity (<i><a href="/wiki/Volksgemeinschaft" title="Volksgemeinschaft">Volksgemeinschaft</a></i>) would replace class division and that "racial comrades" would unite to create a socialist society in the struggle of "proletarian" Germany against
