It is noted that Hitler denounced Germanic paganism in <i>Mein Kampf</i> and condemned Rosenberg\'s and Himmler\'s paganism as "nonsense".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-totalitarianism_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-totalitarianism-208">[208]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Nazi_Germany" title="Economy of Nazi Germany">Economy of Nazi Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_fascism" title="Economics of fascism">Economics of fascism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-15750,_Ausstellung_%22Deutsches_Volk-Deutsche_Arbeit%22.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="536" decoding="async" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-15750%2C_Ausstellung_%22Deutsches_Volk-Deutsche_Arbeit%22.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-15750%2C_Ausstellung_%22Deutsches_Volk-Deutsche_Arbeit%22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-15750%2C_Ausstellung_%22Deutsches_Volk-Deutsche_Arbeit%22.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-15750%2C_Ausstellung_%22Deutsches_Volk-Deutsche_Arbeit%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-15750%2C_Ausstellung_%22Deutsches_Volk-Deutsche_Arbeit%22.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-15750%2C_Ausstellung_%22Deutsches_Volk-Deutsche_Arbeit%22.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-15750,_Ausstellung_%22Deutsches_Volk-Deutsche_Arbeit%22.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Deutsches Volk&#8211;Deutsche Arbeit:</i> German People, German Work (1934) &#8211; an example of <a href="/wiki/Reactionary_modernism" title="Reactionary modernism">reactionary modernism</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Generally speaking, Nazi theorists and politicians blamed Germany\'s previous economic failures on political causes like the influence of Marxism on the workforce, the sinister and exploitative machinations of what they called international Jewry and the vindictiveness of the western political leaders\' <a href="/wiki/World_War_I_reparations" title="World War I reparations">war reparation</a> demands. Instead of traditional economic incentives, the Nazis offered solutions of a political nature, such as the elimination of organised <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unions</a>, rearmament (in contravention of the Versailles Treaty) and biological politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Various work programs designed to establish full-employment for the German population were instituted once the Nazis seized full national power. Hitler encouraged nationally supported projects like the construction of the <i><a href="/wiki/Autobahn" title="Autobahn">Autobahn</a></i> highway system, the introduction of an affordable people\'s car (<i><a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Beetle" title="Volkswagen Beetle">Volkswagen</a></i>) and later the Nazis bolstered the economy through the business and employment generated by military rearmament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> The Nazis benefited early in the regime\'s existence from the first post&#8211;Depression economic upswing, and this combined with their public works projects, job-procurement program and subsidised home repair program reduced unemployment by as much as 40 percent in one year. This development tempered the unfavourable psychological climate caused by the earlier economic crisis and encouraged Germans to march in step with the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upon being appointed Chancellor in 1933, Hitler promised measures to increase employment, protect the German currency, and promote recovery from the Great Depression. These included an agrarian settlement program, labor service, and a guarantee to maintain health care and pensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze200637_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze200637-212">[212]</a></sup> But above all, his priority was rearmament, and the buildup of the German military in preparation for an eventual war to conquer <i><a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a></i> in the East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze200638_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze200638-213">[213]</a></sup> Thus, at the beginning of his rule, Hitler said that &#8220;the future of Germany depends exclusively and only on the reconstruction of the Wehrmacht. All other tasks must cede precedence to the task of rearmament.&#8221;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze200638_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze200638-213">[213]</a></sup> This policy was implemented immediately, with military expenditures quickly growing far larger than the civilian work-creation programs. As early as June 1933, military spending for the year was budgeted to be three times larger than the spending on all civilian work-creation measures in 1932 and 1933 combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze200655_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze200655-214">[214]</a></sup> Nazi Germany increased its military spending faster than any other state in peacetime, with the share of military spending rising from 1 percent to 10 percent of national income in the first two years of the regime alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze200666_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze200666-215">[215]</a></sup> Eventually, by 1944, it reached as high as 75 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In spite of their rhetoric condemning <a href="/wiki/Big_business" title="Big business">big business</a> prior to their rise to power, the Nazis quickly entered into a partnership with German business from as early as February 1933. That month, after being appointed Chancellor but before gaining dictatorial powers, Hitler made a personal appeal to German business leaders to help fund the Nazi Party for the crucial months that were to follow. He argued that they should support him in establishing a dictatorship because "private enterprise cannot be maintained in the age of democracy" and because democracy would allegedly lead to communism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze200699_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze200699-50">[50]</a></sup> He promised to destroy the German left and the trade unions, without any mention of anti-Jewish policies or foreign conquests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze2006100_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze2006100-217">[217]</a></sup> In the following weeks, the Nazi Party received contributions from seventeen different business groups, with the largest coming from <a href="/wiki/IG_Farben" title="IG Farben">IG Farben</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze2006100_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze2006100-217">[217]</a></sup> Historian Adam Tooze writes that the leaders of German business were therefore "willing partners in the destruction of political pluralism in Germany."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze2006101_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze2006101-48">[48]</a></sup> In exchange, owners and managers of German businesses were granted unprecedented powers to control their workforce, <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a> was abolished and wages were frozen at a relatively low level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze2006102_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze2006102-218">[218]</a></sup> Business profits also rose very rapidly, as did corporate investment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETooze2006114_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETooze2006114-219">[219]</a></sup> In addition, the Nazis <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Privatisation" title="Privatisation">privatised</a> public properties and public services, but at the same time they increased economic state control through regulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillebaud_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-guillebaud-220">[220]</a></sup> Hitler believed that private ownership was useful in that it encouraged creative competition and technical innovation, but insisted that it had to conform to national interests and be "productive" rather than "parasitical".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._403_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._403-221">[221]</a></sup> Private property rights were conditional upon following the economic priorities set by the Nazi leadership, with high profits as a reward for firms who followed them and the threat of nationalization being used against those who did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economic573_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-economic573-222">[222]</a></sup> Under Nazi economics, free competition and self-regulating markets diminished, but Hitler\'s <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">social Darwinist</a> beliefs made him retain business competition and private property as economic engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economics_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-economics-223">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-university28_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-university28-224">[224]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Agrarian policies were also important to the Nazis since they corresponded not just to the economy but to their geopolitical conception of <i>Lebensraum</i> as well. For Hitler, the acquisition of land and soil was requisite in moulding the German economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> To tie farmers to their land, selling agricultural land was prohibited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> Farm ownership remained private, but business monopoly rights were granted to marketing boards to control production and prices with a quota system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> The "Hereditary Farm Law of 1933" established a cartel structure under a government body known as the <a href="/wiki/Reichsn%C3%A4hrstand" title="Reichsn&#228;hrstand">Reichsn&#228;hrstand</a> (RNST) which determined "everything from what seeds and fertilizers were used to how land was inherited".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Nazis were hostile to the idea of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_welfare" title="Social welfare">social welfare</a> in principle, upholding instead the social Darwinist concept that the weak and feeble should perish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005483-484_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005483-484-229">[229]</a></sup> They condemned the welfare system of the Weimar Republic as well as private charity, accusing them of supporting people regarded as racially inferior and weak, who should have been weeded out in the process of natural selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005484_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005484-230">[230]</a></sup> Nevertheless, faced with the mass unemployment and poverty of the Great Depression, the Nazis found it necessary to set up charitable institutions to help racially-pure Germans in order to maintain popular support, while arguing that this represented "racial self-help" and not indiscriminate charity or universal social welfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005484-485_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005484-485-231">[231]</a></sup> Thus, Nazi programs such as the <a href="/wiki/Winterhilfswerk" title="Winterhilfswerk">Winter Relief of the German People</a> and the broader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalsozialistische_Volkswohlfahrt" title="Nationalsozialistische Volkswohlfahrt">National Socialist People\'s Welfare</a> (NSV) were organized as quasi-private institutions, officially relying on private donations from Germans to help others of their race - although in practice those who refused to donate could face severe consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005486-487_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005486-487-232">[232]</a></sup> Unlike the social welfare institutions of the Weimar Republic and the Christian charities, the NSV distributed assistance on explicitly racial grounds. It provided support only to those who were "racially sound, capable of and willing to work, politically reliable, and willing and able to reproduce." Non-Aryans were excluded, as well as the "work-shy", "asocials" and the "hereditarily ill."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005489_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005489-233">[233]</a></sup> Successful efforts were made to get middle-class women involved in social work assisting large families,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grunberger46_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grunberger46-166">[166]</a></sup> and the Winter Relief campaigns acted as a ritual to generate public sympathy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richard_Grunberger_p_79_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Grunberger_p_79-234">[234]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hitler primarily viewed the German economy as an instrument of power and believed the economy was not about creating wealth and technical progress so as to improve the quality of life for a nation\'s citizenry, but rather that economic success was paramount for providing the means and material foundations necessary for military conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> While economic progress generated by National Socialist programs had its role in appeasing the German people, the Nazis and Hitler in particular did not believe that economic solutions alone were sufficient to thrust Germany onto the stage as a world power. The Nazis thus sought to secure a general economic revival accompanied by massive military spending for rearmament, especially later through the implementation of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Year_Plan" title="Four Year Plan">Four Year Plan</a>, which consolidated their rule and firmly secured a command relationship between the German arms industry and the National Socialist government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> Between 1933 and 1939, military expenditures were upwards of 82 billion Reichsmarks and represented 23 percent of Germany\'s gross national product as the Nazis mobilised their people and economy for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-communism">Anti-communism</span></h4>\n<p>The Nazis claimed that communism was dangerous to the well-being of nations because of its intention to dissolve <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a>, its support of <a href="/wiki/Class_conflict" title="Class conflict">class conflict</a>, its aggression against the <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a>, its hostility towards small business and its <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated20_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated20-238">[238]</a></sup> Nazism rejected class conflict-based socialism and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_egalitarianism" title="Economic egalitarianism">economic egalitarianism</a>, favouring instead a <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">stratified</a> economy with <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social classes</a> based on merit and talent, retaining <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a> and the creation of national solidarity that transcends class distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated11_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated11-239">[239]</a></sup> Historians <a href="/wiki/Ian_Kershaw" title="Ian Kershaw">Ian Kershaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joachim_Fest" title="Joachim Fest">Joachim Fest</a> argue that in post&#8211;<a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> Germany, the Nazis were one of many nationalist and fascist political parties contending for the leadership of Germany\'s <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communist</a> movement.\n</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i>, Hitler stated his desire to "make war upon the Marxist principle that all men are equal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> He believed that "the notion of equality was a sin against nature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198551_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198551-241">[241]</a></sup> Nazism upheld the "natural inequality of men," including inequality between races and also within each race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198549_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198549-46">[46]</a></sup> The National Socialist state aimed to advance those individuals with special talents or intelligence, so they could rule over the masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198549_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198549-46">[46]</a></sup> Nazi ideology relied on elitism and the <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrerprinzip" title="F&#252;hrerprinzip">F&#252;hrerprinzip</a></i> (leadership principle), arguing that elite minorities should assume leadership roles over the majority, and that the elite minority should itself be organized according to a "hierarchy of talent," with a single leader - the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="F&#252;hrer">F&#252;hrer</a> - at the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198549-50_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198549-50-242">[242]</a></sup> The <i>F&#252;hrerprinzip</i> held that each member of the hierarchy owed absolute obedience to those above him and should hold absolute power over those below him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198550_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198550-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1920s, Hitler urged disparate Nazi factions to unite in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Bolshevism" title="Jewish Bolshevism">Jewish Bolshevism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adolf_Hitler_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adolf_Hitler-243">[243]</a></sup> Hitler asserted that the "three vices" of "Jewish Marxism" were democracy, <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internationalism_(politics)" title="Internationalism (politics)">internationalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitler,_the_Germans,_and_the_Final_Solution_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hitler,_the_Germans,_and_the_Final_Solution-244">[244]</a></sup> The Communist movement, the trade unions, the Social Democratic Party and the left-wing press were all considered to be Jewish-controlled and part of the "international Jewish conspiracy" to weaken the German nation by promoting internal disunity through class struggle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198550_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198550-47">[47]</a></sup> The Nazis also believed that the Jews had instigated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolshevik_revolution" title="Bolshevik revolution">Bolshevik revolution</a> in Russia and that Communists had <a href="/wiki/Stab-in-the-back_myth" title="Stab-in-the-back myth">stabbed Germany in the back</a> and caused it to lose the First World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198552_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198552-245">[245]</a></sup> They further argued that modern cultural trends of the 1920s (such as <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">cubist art</a>) represented "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_Marxism" title="Cultural Marxism">cultural Bolshevism</a>" and were part of a political assault aimed at the spiritual degeneration of the German <i>Volk</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBendersky198552_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBendersky198552-245">[245]</a></sup> Joseph Goebbels published a pamphlet titled <i>The Nazi-Sozi</i> which gave brief points of how National Socialism differed from Marxism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> In 1930, Hitler said: "Our adopted term \'Socialist\' has nothing to do with Marxist Socialism. Marxism is anti-property; true Socialism is not".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-university29_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-university29-247">[247]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">Communist Party of Germany</a> (KPD) was the largest Communist Party in the world outside of the Soviet Union, until it was destroyed by the Nazis in 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> In the 1920s and early 30s, Communists and Nazis often fought each other directly in street violence, with the Nazi paramilitary organizations being opposed by the Communist <a href="/wiki/Roter_Frontk%C3%A4mpferbund" title="Roter Frontk&#228;mpferbund">Red Front</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antifaschistische_Aktion#1930s" title="Antifaschistische Aktion">Anti-Fascist Action</a>. After the beginning of the Great Depression, both Communists and Nazis saw their share of the vote increase. However, while the Nazis were willing to form alliances with other parties of the right, the Communists refused to form an alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party of Germany</a>, the largest party of the left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> After the Nazis came to power, they quickly banned the Communist Party under the allegation that it was preparing for revolution and that it had caused the <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_fire" title="Reichstag fire">Reichstag fire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> Four thousand KPD officials were arrested in February 1933, and by the end of the year 130,000 communists had been sent to concentration camps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the late 1930s and the 1940s, anti-communist regimes and groups that supported Nazism included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falange" title="Falange">Falange</a> in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy regime</a> and the <a href="/wiki/33rd_Waffen_Grenadier_Division_of_the_SS_Charlemagne_(1st_French)" title="33rd Waffen Grenadier Division of the SS Charlemagne (1st French)">33rd Waffen Grenadier Division of the SS Charlemagne (1st French)</a> in France and the <a href="/wiki/British_Union_of_Fascists" title="British Union of Fascists">British Union of Fascists</a> under <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Mosley" title="Oswald Mosley">Sir Oswald Mosley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carroll_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-carroll-252">[252]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-capitalism">Anti-capitalism</span></h4>\n<p>The Nazis argued that <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> damages nations due to <a href="/wiki/International_finance" title="International finance">international finance</a> and the worldwide economic dominance of disloyal <a href="/wiki/Big_business" title="Big business">big business</a>, which they considered to be the product of Jewish influences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated20_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated20-238">[238]</a></sup> Nazi propaganda posters in <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> districts emphasised anti-capitalism, such as one that said: "The maintenance of a rotten industrial system has nothing to do with nationalism. I can love Germany and hate capitalism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publishers30_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-publishers30-253">[253]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both in public and in private, Hitler expressed disdain for capitalism, arguing that it holds nations ransom in the interests of a parasitic <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolitanism" title="Cosmopolitanism">cosmopolitan</a> <a href="/wiki/Rentier_capitalism" title="Rentier capitalism">rentier</a> class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._399_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._399-254">[254]</a></sup> He opposed free market capitalism because it "could not be trusted to put national interests first," and he desired an economy that would direct resources "in ways that matched the many national goals of the regime," such as the buildup of the military, building programs for cities and roads, and economic self-sufficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._403_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._403-221">[221]</a></sup> Hitler also distrusted capitalism for being unreliable due to its <a href="/wiki/Egotism" title="Egotism">egotism</a> and he preferred a state-directed economy that maintains private property and competition but subordinates them to the interests of the <i><a href="/wiki/Volk" title="Volk">Volk</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._399_254-1"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._399-254">[254]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hitler told a party leader in 1934: "The economic system of our day is the creation of the Jews".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._399_254-2"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._399-254">[254]</a></sup> Hitler said to <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> that capitalism had "run its course".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._399_254-3"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._399-254">[254]</a></sup> Hitler also said that the business <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a> "know nothing except their profit. \'Fatherland\' is only a word for them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dictators_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-dictators-255">[255]</a></sup> Hitler was personally disgusted with the ruling bourgeois elites of Germany during the period of the Weimar Republic, who he referred to as "cowardly shits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i>, Hitler effectively supported <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">mercantilism</a> in the belief that economic resources from their respective territories should be seized by force, as he believed that the policy of <i><a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a></i> would provide Germany with such economically valuable territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._402_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._402-257">[257]</a></sup> Hitler argued that the only means to maintain economic security was to have direct control over resources rather than being forced to rely on world trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._402_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._402-257">[257]</a></sup> He claimed that war to gain such resources was the only means to surpass the failing capitalist economic system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R.J._Overy_2004._p._402_257-2"><a href="#cite_note-R.J._Overy_2004._p._402-257">[257]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Joseph Goebbels</a>, who would later go on to become the Nazi Propaganda Minister, was strongly opposed to both capitalism and communism, viewing them as the "two great pillars of materialism" that were "part of the international Jewish conspiracy for world domination."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> Nevertheless, he wrote in his diary in 1925 that if he were forced to choose between them, "in the final analysis", "it would be better for us to go down with Bolshevism than live in eternal slavery under capitalism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disciples_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-disciples-259">[259]</a></sup> He also linked his anti-Semitism to his anti-capitalism, stating in a 1929 pamphlet that "we see, in the Hebrews, the incarnation of capitalism, the misuse of the nation\'s goods."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thosedamnednazis_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-thosedamnednazis-158">[158]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Within the Nazi Party, the faction associated with anti-capitalist beliefs was the <a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">Sturmabteilung</a> (SA), a paramilitary wing led by <a href="/wiki/Ernst_R%C3%B6hm" title="Ernst R&#246;hm">Ernst R&#246;hm</a>. The SA had a complicated relationship with the rest of the party, giving both R&#246;hm himself and local SA leaders significant autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967110-111_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967110-111-260">[260]</a></sup> Different local leaders would even promote different political ideas in their units, including "nationalistic, socialistic, anti-Semitic, racist, v&#246;lkisch, or conservative ideas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967113_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967113-261">[261]</a></sup> There was tension between the SA and Hitler, especially from 1930 onward, as Hitler\'s "increasingly close association with big industrial interests and traditional rightist forces" caused many in the SA to distrust him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967119_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967119-262">[262]</a></sup> The SA regarded Hitler\'s seizure of power in 1933 as a "first revolution" against the left, and some voices within the ranks began arguing for a "second revolution" against the right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967123-124_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967123-124-263">[263]</a></sup> After engaging in violence against the left in 1933, R&#246;hm\'s SA also began attacks against individuals deemed to be associated with conservative reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967123-124;130_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENyomarkay1967123-124;130-39">[39]</a></sup> Hitler saw R&#246;hm\'s independent actions as violating and possibly threatening his leadership, as well as jeopardising the regime by alienating the conservative President <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a> and the conservative-oriented German Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nyomarkay1967_133_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nyomarkay1967_133-40">[40]</a></sup> This resulted in Hitler purging R&#246;hm and other radical members of the SA in 1934, during the <a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Long_Knives" title="Night of the Long Knives">Night of the Long Knives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nyomarkay1967_133_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nyomarkay1967_133-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Totalitarianism">Totalitarianism</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">Totalitarianism</a></div>\n<p>Under Nazism, with its emphasis on the nation, individualism was denounced and instead importance was placed upon Germans belonging to the German <i><a href="/wiki/Volk" title="Volk">Volk</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Volksgemeinschaft#Nazi_Volksgemeinschaft" title="Volksgemeinschaft">"people\'s community"</a> (<i>Volksgemeinschaft)</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> Hitler declared that "every activity and every need of every individual will be regulated by the collectivity represented by the party" and that "there are no longer any free realms in which the individual belongs to himself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fest_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fest-265">[265]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himmler" title="Himmler">Himmler</a> justified the establishment of a repressive police state, in which the security forces could exercise power arbitrarily, by claiming that national security and order should take precedence over the needs of the individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Browder_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-Browder-266">[266]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the famous philosopher and political theorist, <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Hannah Arendt</a>, the allure of Nazism as a totalitarian ideology (with its attendant mobilisation of the German population) resided within the construct of helping that society deal with the cognitive dissonance resultant from the tragic interruption of the First World War and the economic and material suffering consequent to the Depression and brought to order the revolutionary unrest occurring all around them. Instead of the plurality that existed in democratic or parliamentary states, Nazism as a totalitarian system promulgated "clear" solutions to the historical problems faced by Germany, levied support by de-legitimizing the former government of Weimar and provided a politico-biological pathway to a better future, one free from the uncertainty of the past. It was the atomised and disaffected masses that Hitler and the party elite pointed in a particular direction and using clever propaganda to make them into ideological adherents, exploited in bringing Nazism to life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the ideologues of Nazism, <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Nazism_and_Stalinism" title="Comparison of Nazism and Stalinism">much like those of Stalinism</a>, abhorred democratic or parliamentary governance as practiced in the United States or Britain, their differences are substantial. An epistemic crisis occurs when one tries to synthesize and contrast Nazism and Stalinism as two-sides of the same coin with their similarly tyrannical leaders, state-controlled economies and repressive police structures. Namely, while they share a common thematic political construction, they are entirely inimical to one another in their worldviews and when more carefully analysed against one another on a one-to-one level, an "irreconcilable asymmetry" results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Reactionary_or_revolutionary.3F"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactionary_or_revolutionary?">Reactionary or revolutionary?</span></h2>\n<p>Although Nazism is often seen as a reactionary movement, it did not seek a return of Germany to the pre-Weimar monarchy, but instead looked much further back to a mythic halcyon Germany which never existed. It has also been seen &#8211; as it was by the German-American scholar <a href="/wiki/Franz_Leopold_Neumann" title="Franz Leopold Neumann">Franz Leopold Neumann</a> &#8211; as the result of a crisis of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> which manifested as a "totalitarian monopoly capitalism". In this view Nazism is a mass movement of the middle class which was in opposition to a mass movement of workers in <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> and its extreme form, <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">Communism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBracher197019-20_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBracher197019-20-269">[269]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Karl_Dietrich_Bracher" title="Karl Dietrich Bracher">Karl Dietrich Bracher</a>, however, argues that,\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Such an interpretation runs the risk of misjudging the revolutionary component of National Socialism, which cannot be dismissed as being simply reactionary. Rather, from the very outset, and particularly as it developed into the <a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">SS</a> state, National Socialism aimed at a transformation of state and society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBracher197019-20_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBracher197019-20-269">[269]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>and that, Hitler\'s and the Nazi Party\'s political positions\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>were of a revolutionary nature: destruction of existing political and social structures and their supporting elites; profound dispain for civic order, for human and moral values, for Habsburg and Hohenzollern, for liberal and Marxist ideas. The middle class and middle-class values, bourgeois nationalism and capitalism, the professionals, the intelligentsia and the upper class were dealt the sharpest rebuff.  These were the groups which had to be uprooted...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBracher1970165_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBracher1970165-270">[270]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>After the failure of the <a href="/wiki/Beer_Hall_Putsch" title="Beer Hall Putsch">Beer Hall Putsch</a> in 1923, and his subsequent trial and imprisonment, Hitler decided that the way for the Nazi Party to achieve power was not through insurrection, but through legal and quasi-legal means.  This did not sit well with the brown-shirted stormtroopers of the <a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">SA</a>, especially those in Berlin, who chafed under the restrictions that Hitler placed on them, and their subordination to the party.  This resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Stennes_Revolt" title="Stennes Revolt">Stennes Revolt</a> of 1930-31, after which Hitler made himself the Supreme Commander of the SA, and brought <a href="/wiki/Ernst_R%C3%B6hm" title="Ernst R&#246;hm">Ernst R&#246;hm</a> back to be their Chief of Staff and keep them in line. The quashing of the SA\'s revolutionary fervor convinced many businessmen and military leaders that the Nazis had put aside their insurrectionist past, and that Hitler could be a reliable partner  <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBracher1970231-32_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBracher1970231-32-271">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003274_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003274-272">[272]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, after the Nazis\' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machtergreifung" title="Machtergreifung">"Seizure of Power"</a> in 1933, R&#246;hm and the Brown Shirts were not content for the party to simply carry the reigns of power.  Instead, they pressed for a continuation of the "National Socialist revolution" to bring about sweeping social changes, which Hitler, primarily for tactical reasons, was not willing to do at that time.  He was instead focused on rebuilding the military and reorienting the economy to provide the rearmament necessary for invasion of the countries to the east of Germany, especially Poland and Russia, to get the <i><a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a></i> ("living space") he believed was necessary to the survival of the Aryan race. For this, he needed the co-operation of not only the military, but also the vital organs of capitalism, the banks and big businesses, which he would be unlikely to get if Germany\'s social and economic structure was being radically overhauled.  R&#246;hm\'s public proclamation that the SA would not allow the "German Revolution" to be halted or undermined caused Hitler to announce that "The revolution is not a permanent condition."  The unwillingness of R&#246;hm and the SA to cease their agitation for a "Second Revolution", and the unwarranted fear of a "R&#246;hm putsch" to accomplish it, were factors behind Hitler\'s purging of the SA leadership in the <a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Long_Knives" title="Night of the Long Knives">Night of the Long Knives</a> in July 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999501-503_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999501-503-273">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBracher1970300-02_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBracher1970300-02-274">[274]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite such tactical breaks necessitated by pragmatic concerns, which were typical for Hitler during his rise to power and in the early years of his regime, Hitler never ceased being a revolutionary dedicated to the radical transformation of Germany, especially when it concerned racial matters. In his monograph, <i>Hitler: Study of a Revolutionary?</i>, <a href="/wiki/Martyn_Housden" title="Martyn Housden">Martyn Housden</a> concludes:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>[Hitler] compiled a most extensive set of revolutionary goals (calling for radical social and political change); he mobilized a revolutionary following so extensive and powerful that many of his aims were achieved; he established and ran a dictatorial revolutionary state; and he disseminated his ideas abroad through a revolutionary foreign policy and war.  In short, he defined and controlled the National Socialist revolution in all its phases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Of course, there were aspects of Nazism which were reactionary, such as their attitude toward the role of women in society, which was completely traditionalist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBracher1970179_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBracher1970179-276">[276]</a></sup> calling for the return of women to the home as wives, mothers and homemakers, although ironically this ideological policy was undermined in reality by the growing labor shortages and need for more workers. The number of women in the workplace climbed throughout the period of Nazi control of Germany, from 4.24 million in 1933 to 4.52 million in 1936 and 5.2 million in 1938, numbers that far exceeded those of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBracher1970421-22_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBracher1970421-22-277">[277]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another reactionary aspect of Nazism was in their arts policy, which stemmed from Hitler\'s rejection of all forms of <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_art" title="Degenerate art">"degenerate"</a> <a href="/wiki/Modern_art" title="Modern art">modern art</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/20th_century_classical_music" title="20th century classical music">music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw199982_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw199982-278">[278]</a></sup> Overall, however, Nazism &#8211; being the ideology and practices of the Nazi Party, and the Nazi Party being the manifestation of Hitler\'s will<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBracher1970191_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBracher1970191-279">[279]</a></sup>  &#8211; is best seen as essentially revolutionary in nature.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_Nazism">Post-war Nazism</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neo-Nazism" title="Neo-Nazism">Neo-Nazism</a></div>\n<p>Following <a href="/wiki/End_of_World_War_II_in_Europe" title="End of World War II in Europe">Nazi Germany\'s defeat in World War II</a> and the end of <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>, overt expressions of support for Nazi ideas were prohibited in Germany and other European countries. Nonetheless, movements which self-identify as National Socialist or which are described as adhering to National Socialism continue to exist on the fringes of politics in many western societies. Usually espousing a <a href="/wiki/White_supremacy" title="White supremacy">white supremacist</a> <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a>, many deliberately adopt the symbols of Nazi Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Nazism_and_Stalinism" title="Comparison of Nazism and Stalinism">Comparison of Nazism and Stalinism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Consequences_of_Nazism" title="Consequences of Nazism">Consequences of Nazism</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fascism_in_the_United_States" title="Fascism in the United States">Fascism in the United States</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Functionalism_versus_intentionalism" title="Functionalism versus intentionalism">Functionalism versus intentionalism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Italian_Fascism" title="Italian Fascism">Italian fascism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_books_about_Nazi_Germany" title="List of books about Nazi Germany">List of books about Nazi Germany</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi_occultism" title="Nazi occultism">Nazi occultism</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Political_views_of_Adolf_Hitler" title="Political views of Adolf Hitler">Political views of Adolf Hitler</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<p><b>Notes</b>\n</p>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-Jones2003-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Jones2003_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Jones_(phonetician)" title="Daniel Jones (phonetician)">Jones, Daniel</a> (2003) [1917]. book">Baum, Bruce David (2006). <i>The Rise and Fall of the Caucasian Race: A Political History of Racial Identity</i>. "The Reception of Luther\'s Writings on the Jews from the Reformation to the End of the 19th Century", <i>Lutheran Quarterly</i>, n.s. 1 (Spring 1987) 1:72&#8211;97. Wallmann writes: "The assertion that Luther\'s expressions of anti-Jewish sentiment have been of major and persistent influence in the centuries after the Reformation, and that there exists a continuity between Protestant anti-Judaism and modern racially oriented anti-Semitism, is at present wide-spread in the literature; since the Second World War it has understandably become the prevailing opinion." * Michael, Robert. <i>Holy Hatred: Christianity, Antisemitism, and the Holocaust</i>. New York: Palgrave Macmillan, 2006; see chapter 4 "The Germanies from Luther to Hitler", pp. 105&#8211;151. * Hillerbrand, Hans J. "Martin Luther," <i>Encyclop&#230;dia Britannica</i>, 2007. Hillerbrand writes: "[H]is strident pronouncements against the Jews, especially toward the end of his life, have raised the question of whether Luther significantly encouraged the development of German anti-Semitism. Although many scholars have taken this view, this perspective puts far too much emphasis on Luther and not enough on the larger peculiarities of German history."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-baylor-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-baylor_203-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Marc_H._Ellis" title="Marc H.
